                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()
        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201703951')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="309958f5032849a7a6a626296baa21c7"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="309958f5032849a7a6a626296baa21c7">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '290bdea1-5e28-4fc8-8525-63aa67c643f3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'DED1674962590FD6EF98E6A2A8F10017';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d5163602" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The dual-ion battery (DIB) system has attracted great attention owing to its merits of low cost, high energy, and environmental friendliness. However, the DIBs based on sodium-ion electrolytes are seldom reported due to the lack of appropriate anode materials for reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. Herein, a new sodium-ion based DIB named as MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB using penne-like MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotube as anode and expanded graphite as cathode is constructed and optimized for the first time. The hierarchical MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotube provides expanded (002) interlayer spacing of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, which facilitates fast Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reaction kinetics, thus contributing to improved DIB performance. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB delivers a reversible capacity of 65 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C in the voltage window of 1.0–4.0 V, with good cycling performance for 200 cycles and 85% capacity retention, indicating the feasibility of potential applications for sodium-ion based DIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703951-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703951-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have become the most widely used energy storage devices in the field of portable electronic devices, electric vehicles, smart grids, etc. However, the current LIB technique becomes difficult to satisfy the ever-growing markets due to the limited and unevenly distributed lithium resource.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0001_R_d5163594e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature Nat. Chem. Nat. Commun. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0002_R_d5163594e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></span> Recently, a novel battery system called dual-ion battery (DIB), has attracted increased attention owing to its merits of high voltage, low cost, and environmental friendliness. Actually, in 1938, Rudorff and Hofmann first discovered the phenomenon of anion intercalation into graphite cathode for electrochemical energy storage systems.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0003_R_d5163594e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Z. Anorg. Allg. Chem.">3</a></span> In 1989, the concept of “dual-ion battery” consisting of two graphite electrodes and a nonaqueous electrolyte was first presented, where graphite was demonstrated to be a redox-amphoteric material that allows both the anions and cations to intercalate/deintercalate into/from its layers.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0004_R_d5163594e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></span> Afterward, progresses on DIBs have been made in the past few decades. Various kinds of electrolytes,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0005_R_d5163594e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Power Sources J. Electrochem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc.">5</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0006_R_d5163594e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Commun. Adv. Mater.">6</a></span> cathode materials including graphitic carbon materials,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0007_R_d5163594e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources Electrochem. Solid-State Lett. J. Power Sources Electrochim. Acta">7</a></span> and metal–organic framework<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0008_R_d5163594e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a></span> have been investigated. For anode materials, except for graphite<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0006_R_d5163594e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Commun. Adv. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#smll201703951-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0009_R_d5163594e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></span> and some metal oxides,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0010_R_d5163594e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">10</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0011_R_d5163594e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Power Sources J. Power Sources">11</a></span> our group recently developed low-cost metallic materials (Al, Sn, etc.) as both anode material and current collector,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0012_R_d5163594e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>-<a href="#smll201703951-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0014_R_d5163594e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span> which significantly simplified the structure of DIB and reduced cost, making this kind of DIBs more promising for application in large-scale energy storage. However, most of the reported DIBs are mainly based on Li-ion electrolyte, which hinders their long-term development taking into account of the limited and unevenly distributed lithium resource.</p>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted more and more attention in the past decades owing to the merits of low cost and large earth abundance.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0015_R_d5163594e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></span> However, sodium-ion based DIBs were rarely investigated<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0014_R_d5163594e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0016_R_d5163594e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochim. Acta">16</a>-<a href="#smll201703951-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0018_R_d5163594e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></span> due to the larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.4 times larger than Li<sup>+</sup>),<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0019_R_d5163594e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">19</a></span> leading to failure of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation into/from graphite anode. Therefore, designing appropriate anode materials plays an important role on developing high-performance Na-DIBs. As a typical transition metal sulfide, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) has a 2D layered structure, which offers permeable channels for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, leading to high theoretical Na storage capacity of 670 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0020_R_d5163594e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">20</a></span> Nevertheless, bare MoS<sub>2</sub> materials usually undergo unsatisfying electrochemical performance due to the intrinsic poor conductivity and severe aggregation during charging/discharging. Therefore, design of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanocomposites by nanosizing and combining with conductive component should be an effective strategy to improve its electrochemical performance for the optimized conductivity and structural stability,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0021_R_d5163594e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano">21</a></span> especially various MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanostructures.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0022_R_d5163594e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano Small Small Small J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report a novel MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-graphite DIB (named as MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB) based on a sodium-ion electrolyte, employing a penne-like MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposite as anode and expanded graphite as cathode for the first time. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposite has a hierarchical penne-like nanotube structure with carbon homogenously distributed in the nanocomposite, in which the (002) interlayer spacing was enlarged. Owing to the above synergistic effect, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB was demonstrated to deliver a reversible discharge capacity of 65 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C (1 C corresponding to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) over the voltage range of 1.0–4.0 V, which also exhibited good cycling stability for over 200 cycles with 85% capacity retention. The design of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB paves a new avenue for developing high-performance and low-cost energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703951-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703951-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The penne-like MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes was prepared by a two-step microwave-solvothermal and the following carbonization method, schematically shown in Figure S1 in the Supporting Information. First, sulfur powder and MoO<sub>3</sub> were dissolved in octylamine solvent, where the sulfur and MoO<sub>3</sub> act as the S and Mo precursor respectively, and octylamine acts as the carbon precursor. Then absolute ethanol was added and stirred for 5 min to obtain a light yellow suspension. The solution was transferred into a specially designed quartz tube, and put into a microwave synthesizer, then heated up to 190 °C within 1 min and kept at 190 °C for 30 min, and finally cooled down to 50 °C. The black precipitate was collected by centrifugation and washed with ethanol and distilled water for several times, and then dried in vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h. Finally, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite was obtained by heating the sample in a tube furnace at 700 °C for 1 h under a mixed Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (Ar:H<sub>2</sub> = 95:5) atmosphere. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>a presents the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C samples before and after annealing. First, two distinct diffraction peaks of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C sample at 2θ = 33.3° and 56.9° were observed, which can be indexed to (100) and (110) planes of hexagonal 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDF 37–1492), respectively. A more interesting thing is that peaks at 9.0° (<i>d</i> = 0.98 nm) and 18° (<i>d</i> = 0.49 nm) with a relationship of doubling interplanar distance (0.98 nm = 0.49 nm × 2) were observed corresponding to expanded (002) and (004) planes of MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0023_R_d5163594e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></span> The series of (00<i>l</i>) Bragg peaks are originated from layered structure. In addition, the shifted (002) peak to low 2θ degree compared with the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> manifests the large expanded interlayer distance from 0.615 to 0.98 nm. Before annealing, the strong (001) peak at 4.5° appeared, which maybe originated from disordered and hybrid 2H and 1T structures and also from the octylamine-intercalation-induced change in structural factor by lattice expansion.[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0020_R_d5163594e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">20</a></span>] Figure S2 in the Supporting Information shows the scanning electron microscope (SEM) images of the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes before annealing, which can be clearly seen that the surface of the nanotubes is extra coarse and there is no obvious layered structure. After annealing, the (001) diffraction peak disappeared, while the (002) and (004) diffraction peaks became stronger, suggesting the obvious layered structure of the annealed MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes. The broad diffraction peak from 20° to 25° corresponds to the (002) diffraction of the graphitic layer-by-layer structure, further illustrated the existence of carbon. The carbon content in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite was determined by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>b, which was calculated to be about 12.6 wt% according to Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>b. To further demonstrate the structure of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite, Raman spectroscopy of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C sample was performed and the result is shown in Figure S4 in the Supporting Information. It shows two peaks located respectively at around 375 and 402 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> mode originated from the Mo-S in-plane vibration and the A<sub>1g</sub> mode from out-of-plane vibration, respectively. In addition, two weak and broad peaks at 1360 and 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to D and G band were also observed, respectively, indicating the presence of amorphous disordered carbon, consistent well with the XRD result.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703951-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fc2fb597-3ecc-406b-8635-1e2c16e0f5d4/smll201703951-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fc2fb597-3ecc-406b-8635-1e2c16e0f5d4/smll201703951-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5eeb8040-c5cc-4405-8b94-98ffbecc0085/smll201703951-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fc2fb597-3ecc-406b-8635-1e2c16e0f5d4/smll201703951-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703951-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703951" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes before and after annealing and the bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> for comparison. b) TGA curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite and bare MoS<sub>2</sub> measured under air atmosphere. c,d) SEM images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes after annealing with different magnification. e,f) TEM and HRTEM images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotube after annealing. g) TEM EDX mapping images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotube with Mo, S, and C elements.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite was further investigated. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>c,d shows the SEM images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposite, which has a penne-like 1D nanotube structure with hollow interior and corrugated exterior. The average length of the nanotube is ≈1.5 µm, with internal diameter of around 200 nm and wall thickness of around 100 nm. Compared with the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C sample before annealing (Figure S2, Supporting Information), the length of the annealed MoS<sub>2</sub>/C is shorter resulted from the shrinkage of the nanotubes during the thermal treatment. In addition, the internal diameter is larger due to the remove of the redundant octylamine and carbide during annealing. The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>e further confirms that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes have a hierarchical penne-like structure with hollow interior and wall assembled by nanosheets. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>f and Figure S3 (Supporting Information) further reveal that the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets exhibit distinct multilayered structures with an increased interlayer spacing of ≈0.98 nm that corresponds to the expanded (002) plane of the MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>f) and interlayer spacing of ≈0.27 nm that corresponds to the (100) plane of the MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure S3, Supporting Information), which are consistent with the XRD result in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>a. It can also been seen from Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>f that the carbon layer was coated outside of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite and also in between the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers with amorphous state. Such MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes with enlarged interlayer spacing are beneficial for reversible insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> and fast ion transportation in SIBs, which indicates good electrochemical performance for Na storage. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0001">1</a>g further show the TEM energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping profiles of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotube with Mo, S, and C elements, with the three elements uniformly distributed throughout the nanotube, indicating that the carbon was distributed homogenously in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite, which is beneficial for improved electrical conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite before and after annealing were further analyzed (Figure S5, Supporting Information). Before annealing, the sample shows two peaks located at 233.8 and 230.7 eV in the Mo 3d spectrum (Figure S5a, Supporting Information), which are attributed to Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively, characteristic of Mo<sup>4+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>. After annealing, the positions of the two Mo 3d peaks shifted to lower binding energy (232.3 and 229.2 eV), which is due to decreased contamination of negatively charged species after annealing.[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0021_R_d5163594e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano">21</a></span>] In addition, another small peak located at 226.4 eV was corresponding to S 2s peak. Figure S5b in the Supporting Information shows the binding energies of S 2p peaks, which locate at 162.5 eV for S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 161.2 eV for S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, characteristic of S<sup>2−</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>. After annealing, the S 2p peaks strengthened and slightly shifted higher to 163.2 and 162.0 eV, respectively. For the C 1s spectrum (Figure S5c, Supporting Information), the unannealed sample shows main peaks at 284.6 and 286.0 eV assigned to C<span class="icomoon"></span>C and C<span class="icomoon"></span>O respectively,[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0022_R_d5163594e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Nano Small Small Small J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></span>] indicating the formation of amorphous carbon after microwave solvothermal reaction. After annealing, the intensity of the C 1s peaks obviously decreased with slightly lower shift, indicating the decrease of the carbon content due to the vanish of the organic residue after annealing treatment.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composite were first evaluated by Na-MoS<sub>2</sub>/C half-cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0002">2</a>a shows cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode for the first five cycles with a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. In the initial cycle, two broad peaks at 1.2–2.5 and 0.5–1.2 V was observed in the discharge curve, which were assigned to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> interlayers and the continuous Na<sup>+</sup> insertion induced phase transition from 2H to 1T Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0024_R_d5163594e689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Nano ACS Nano">24</a></span> During charging process, the corresponding two broad oxidation peaks at 0.5–1.2 and 1.2–2.5 V were also observed, indicating the reverse Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process. In the following five cycles, the charge/discharge curves were almost overlapped, indicating good electrochemical reversibility of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703951-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/13e6b108-b438-4def-8e2a-ead8f7b15b80/smll201703951-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/13e6b108-b438-4def-8e2a-ead8f7b15b80/smll201703951-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/926cf9e3-424a-4a14-bdf1-65467b4f363b/smll201703951-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/13e6b108-b438-4def-8e2a-ead8f7b15b80/smll201703951-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703951-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703951" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the Na-MoS<sub>2</sub>/C half-cell. a) CV profiles of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the initial 5 cycles. b) Charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode at different current rates from 0.15 C to 1.0 C in the potential range of 0.01–3 V (1 C = 670 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>). c) Corresponding charge/discharge specific capacities at different current rates. d) Cycling performance of the Na-MoS<sub>2</sub>/C half- cell at a current rate of 0.3 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0002">2</a>b shows typical discharge/charge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode at different current rates from 0.15 to 1 C (1 C = 670 mA g<sup>−1</sup> according to the theoretical capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>). The discharge/charge curves are similar at various current rates with two slopes at 1.3–2.5 and 0.6–1.3 V in all discharge curves and two slopes at 0.2–1.0 and 1.5–2.5 V in all charge curves, which are in accordance with the CV results. In addition, the discharge/charge voltage plateaus separated slowly when the current rate increased, indicating small electrochemical polarization. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0002">2</a>c displays the rate performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, which delivered reversible capacity of 345, 318, 300, 280, and 265 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.15, 0.3, 0.5, 0.8, and 1.0 C, respectively. When the current rate gradually decreased to 0.15 C, the capacity recovered to the initial value, indicating an excellent reversibility and recyclability. Furthermore, long-term cycling stability of the Na-MoS<sub>2</sub>/C half-cell was performed at 0.3 C, as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0002">2</a>d. The electrode exhibited an initial discharge/charge capacity of 577/449 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively with a coulombic efficiency of 78%. Although they decreased to 349/333 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the second cycle, during which the irreversible capacity loss was attributed to the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film on the surface of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode and decomposition of electrolyte, the SEI film could protect the electrode material from structure damage. Accordingly, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode shows excellent capacity stability with reversible capacity of 315 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (94.6% retention) after 200 cycles and coulombic efficiency above 99%.</p>
         
         <p>By employing the above MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes with excellent Na storage performance as the anode material and expanded graphite as the cathode material, we assembled a novel DIB configuration named as MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB based on 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/(ethylene carbonate : ethyl methyl carbonate : dimethyl carbonate = 1:1:1 v/v/v) electrolyte and tested the electrochemical performance. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>a shows the schematic illustration of the charge/discharge mechanism of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB. During the charging process, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions are intercalated into graphite cathode to form C<i><sub>x</sub></i>(PF<sub>6</sub>),[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0007_R_d5163594e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources Electrochem. Solid-State Lett. J. Power Sources Electrochim. Acta">7</a></span>] meanwhile the Na<sup>+</sup> cations are intercalated into the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode to form Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoS<sub>2</sub>. During the discharging process, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and Na<sup>+</sup> cations were deintercalated from the graphite cathode and the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode and returned to the electrolyte, respectively. First of all, charge/discharge curves of graphite-Na half-cell and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-Na half-cell were performed to investigate the insertion/extraction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in graphite and Na<sup>+</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes. As shown in Figure S6 in the Supporting Information, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion/extraction in graphite occurs at high working potential (3.0–5.0 V), while the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction occurs at low working potential (below 2.5 V), contributing to high working voltage of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G full cell. Typical galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB in the working voltage range of 1.0–4.0 V at 2 C (1 C corresponding to be 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> according to the graphite cathode) are shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>b. The charge/discharge curves of the full cell are different from those of the half-cells, but reflected the linear superposition of the curves of the half-cells. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>c shows the corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> differential profile of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB during charging and discharging. The charge curve consisted of three regions of 2.5–3.1 V (stage I), 3.1–3.5 V (stage II), and 3.5–4.0 V (stage III), mainly corresponding to different stages of anion intercalation into the graphite cathode.[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0007_R_d5163594e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources Electrochem. Solid-State Lett. J. Power Sources Electrochim. Acta">7</a></span>] Three anion deintercalation stages can also be identified in the discharging curve ranging from 4.0 to 2.6 V (stage III′), 2.6 to 2.0 V (stage II′), and 2.2 to 1.8 V (stage I′), respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703951-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0c8759fc-08d1-48a3-8110-b6a2f2e9c48a/smll201703951-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0c8759fc-08d1-48a3-8110-b6a2f2e9c48a/smll201703951-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ebb74076-e27a-46db-a6e9-c37b04ed56c0/smll201703951-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0c8759fc-08d1-48a3-8110-b6a2f2e9c48a/smll201703951-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703951-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703951" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the working mechanism of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB wherein PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> ions intercalate into the graphite cathode and Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalate into the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode during the charging process. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB at 2 C. c) Corresponding d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> differential curves of the battery. d,e) Ex situ XRD patterns of the graphite cathode and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode at different charge/discharge voltages in the initial cycle at 2 C. f) SEM EDX mapping images of a fully-charged MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the working mechanism of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB, further characterizations of the electrodes were performed. The insertion/extraction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in graphite cathode and the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode during the charge/discharge process were conducted by ex situ XRD analyses. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>d shows the XRD patterns of the graphite cathode at different charge/discharge voltages in the initial cycle. The pristine expanded graphite shows a sharp (002) diffraction peak at 26.4°, indicating a well-ordered graphite structure. During charging state, the intensity of (002) peak greatly weakened and gradually split into two peaks, with a new broad (00n+1) peak at lower degree generated caused by the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> intercalation.[<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0007_R_d5163594e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Power Sources Electrochem. Solid-State Lett. J. Power Sources Electrochim. Acta">7</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0025_R_d5163594e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Chem. C">25</a></span>] When the charging voltage increased, the intensity ratio of the (00n+1) and (002) peak increased accompanied with peak shift to lower 2θ value (25.4° at high cut-off voltage of 4.0 V), suggesting continuous PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> intercalation into graphite cathode. During discharging process, the (00n+1) peak at around 25.4° gradually weakened and shifted to higher degree. When discharged to 1.0 V, the two peaks merged into one peak but with little pimpling peak at around 25.8° and the (002) peak also weakened compared with that of the pristine graphite, which could be attributed to partially trapped PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> in the graphite cathode as well as some Na<sup>+</sup> loss in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode after the initial cycle. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>e shows the ex situ XRD profiles of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode at different charge/discharge voltages in the initial cycle. During charging/discharging, the peaks maintained almost the same to the pristine anode with no obvious peak change. However, SEM EDX mapping images of the charged MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode (Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0003">3</a>f) clearly show the existence of Na element, which could be attributed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> during charging. The little change of the XRD peaks of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode may be due to the fact that the Na<sup>+</sup> ions were shallowly intercalated into the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode due to the much higher specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode than graphite cathode. In addition, the enlarged interplanar distance (0.98 nm) of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C offered enough space to insert/extract Na<sup>+</sup> with little deformation.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB was thoroughly investigated, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>a,b presents rate performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB based on the electrolyte of 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/EMC/DMC (1:1:1 v/v/v) at various current rates from 2 to 10 C over a voltage window of 1.0–4.0 V. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB showed reversible discharge capacity of 65, 53, 49, 45, and 35 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2, 3, 4, 5, and 10 C, respectively, revealing good rate performance. In contrast, the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB using bulk MoS<sub>2</sub> showed poor rate performance with severely decreased capacity at high current rates, which only delivered 11 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C. In addition, the corresponding charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB (Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>b) under different current rates are similar with moderate increase of charge/discharge voltage separation, indicating small electrochemical polarization of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB. Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>c shows the Nyquist plots of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB after 50 cycles at 2 C. It can be clearly seen that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB showed much smaller semicircle than the MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB, indicating a much smaller charge transfer resistance,<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0026_R_d5163594e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></span> which was attributed to the enhanced conductivity and fast ion diffusion channel of the hierarchical nanotube structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode, thus favorable for high rate capability and good cycling stability. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>d, the charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB at 50th, 100th, and 200th cycle at current rate of 2 C almost overlapped with little polarization. Furthermore, Figure <a href="#smll201703951-fig-0004">4</a>e shows the long-term cycling stability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB at 2 C for 200 cycles with MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB as contrast. The discharge capacity remained stable with a capacity of 55 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles and 85% capacity retention, demonstrating good cycling performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB, which is superior than most reported DIBs based on sodium-ion electrolytes.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0016_R_d5163594e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Electrochim. Acta">16</a>, <a href="#smll201703951-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0018_R_d5163594e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></span> The coulombic efficiency also kept at around 90% during 200 cycles apart from the first several cycles. In contrast, the discharge capacity of the DIB based on bare MoS<sub>2</sub> anode faded quickly after 85 cycles, and only remained 15 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, demonstrating the significant role of the hierarchical structure with carbon layer on improving the cycling performance. Figure S7a,b in the Supporting Information shows the SEM images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode in the DIB before charging/discharging and after 200 cycles. It can be seen that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanotubes were dispersed in the acetylene black and obvious layer structure was observed. After long cycles, no distinct deformation and collapse of the nanotubes were observed, illustrating good structure stability. The surface of the nanotubes became smooth due to the formation of the protective SEI film. EDX elemental mapping (Figure S7c, Supporting Information) of the charged MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode at the 200th cycle clearly shows the presence of the C, O, and F elements, which are common composing elements of SEI film.<span><a href="#smll201703951-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201703951-bib-0027_R_d5163594e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">27</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201703951-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebfee18c-c641-4176-b6a7-d25c2df20be9/smll201703951-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebfee18c-c641-4176-b6a7-d25c2df20be9/smll201703951-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4bb54c8e-f2c0-43ea-abc7-4c7777012597/smll201703951-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebfee18c-c641-4176-b6a7-d25c2df20be9/smll201703951-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201703951-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201703951" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Discharge capacities of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB as contrast at various current rates from 2 C to 10 C. b) Corresponding charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB at various current rates. c) Nyquist plots of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB and MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB measured after 50 cycles. d) Charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB after 50th, 100th, and 200th cycle at 2C. e) Long-term cycling stability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB at 2 C for 200 cycles with MoS<sub>2</sub>-G DIB as contrast.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201703951-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201703951-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a sodium-based dual-ion battery using penne-like MoS<sub>2</sub>/C hierarchical nanotube as anode material has been constructed in this work. The as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanocomposite can supply a significantly expanded (002) interlayer spacing with 0.98 nm of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, thus beneficial for fast insertion/extraction kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>. As a result, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB delivered a reversible capacity of 65 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C in the voltage window of 1.0–4.0 V, and exhibited good cycling performance for 200 cycles with 85% capacity retention. All these results indicate that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-G DIB has promising potential for large-scale energy storage applications such as smart grids.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
